his book is what its author believes to be the only attempt yet made to
put the nglish reader into possession, in clear, compact, and what it

the lectures and essays of scholars to learned societies. he reader to
whom this literature is entirely new is introduced, as it were, to
numerous people of whose antecedents he knows nothing and the effect is
often disconcerting enough to make him lay down the volume in despair.

ut here he will at last make the formal acuaintance of all the chief

moreâ€”and, indeed, no lessâ€”than an endeavour to refresh the vitality of
nglish poetry at its most ancient native fount.

he book does not, of course, profess to be for Celtic scholars, to
whom, indeed, its author himself owes all that is within it. It aims
only at interesting the reader familiar with the mythologies of Greece,

his hope that, in choosing from the considerable, though scattered,

he has chosen wisely, and that his readers will be able, should they

o acknowledge, as far as possible, such debts is now the authorâ€s

It should hardly be necessary to remind the reader of what profound
interest and value to every nation are its earliest legendary and
poetical records. he beautiful myths of Greece form a sufficing
example. In threefold manner, they have influenced the destiny of the
people that created them, and of the country of which they were the
imagined theatre. irst, in the ages in which they were still fresh,
belief and pride in them were powerful enough to bring scattered tribes
into confederation. econdly, they gave the inspiration to sculptor and
poet of an art and literature unsurpassed, if not uneualled, by any
other age or race. astly, when â€the glory that was Greeceâ€ had faded,
and her people had, by dint of successive invasions, perhaps even ceased
to have any right to call themselves ellenes, they have passed over
into the literatures of the modern world, and so given to Greece herself
a poetic interest that still makes a petty kingdom of greater account in
the eyes of its compeers than many others far superior to it in extent
and resources.

his permeating influence of the Greek poetical mythology, apparent in
all civilized countries, has acted especially upon our own. rom almost
the very dawn of nglish literature, the Greek stories of gods and
heroes have formed a large part of the stockintrade of nglish poets.
he inhabitants of lympus occupy, under their betterknown atin names,
almost as great a space in nglish poetry as they did in that of the
countries to which they were native. rom Chaucer downwards, they have
captivated the imagination alike of the poets and their hearers. he
magic cauldron of classic myth fed, like the Celtic â€Grailâ€, all who
came to it for sustenance.

At last, however, its potency became somewhat exhausted. Alien and
exotic to nglish soil, it degenerated slowly into a convention. In the
shallow hands of the poetasters of the eighteenth century, its figures
became mere puppets. With every wood a â€groveâ€, and every rustic maid a
â€nymphâ€, one could only expect to find enus armed with patch and
powderpuff, Mars shouldering a musket, and Apollo inspiring the
versifierâ€s own trivial strains. he affectation killedâ€”and fortunately
killedâ€”a mode of expression which had become obsolete. mothered by ust
ridicule, and abandoned to the commonplace vocabulary of the inferior
hackwriter, classic myth became a subect which only the greatest poets
could afford to handle.

ut mythology is of such vital need to literature that, deprived of the
store of legend native to southern urope, imaginative writers looked
for a fresh impulse. hey turned their eyes to the orth. Inspiration
was sought, not from lympus, but from Asgard. Moreover, it was believed
that the fount of primeval poetry issuing from candinavian and eutonic
myth was truly our own, and that we were rightful heirs of it by reason
of the Angloaxon in our blood. And so, indeed, we are but it is not
our sole heritage. here must also run much Celticâ€”that is, truly
ritishâ€”blood in our veins. And Matthew Arnold was probably right in
asserting that, while we owe to the Angloaxon the more practical
ualities that have built up the ritish mpire, we have inherited from
the Celtic side that poetic vision which has made nglish literature the
most brilliant since the Greek.2

We have the right, therefore, to enter upon a new spiritual possession.
And a splendid one it is he Celtic mythology has little of the heavy
crudeness that repels one in eutonic and candinavian story. It is as
beautiful and graceful as the Greek and, unlike the Greek, which is the
reflection of a clime and soil which few of us will ever see, it is our
own. ivinities should, surely, seem the inevitable outgrowth of the
land they move in ow strange Apollo would appear, naked among
icebergs, or furclad hor striding under groves of palms ut the
Celtic gods and heroes are the natural inhabitants of a ritish
landscape, not seeming foreign and outofplace in a scene where there
is no vine or olive, but â€shading in withâ€ our homely oak and bracken,
gorse and heath.

hus we gain an altogether fresh interest in the beautiful spots of our
own islands, especially those of the wilder and more mountainous west,
where the older inhabitants of the land lingered longest. axon conuest
obliterated much in astern ritain, and changed more but in the West
of ngland, in Wales, in cotland, and especially in legendhaunted
Ireland, the hills and dales still keep memories of the ancient gods of
the ancient race. ere and there in outh Wales and the West of ngland
are regionsâ€”once mysterious and still romanticâ€”which the ritish Celts
held to be the homes of gods or outposts of the ther World. In Ireland,
not only is there scarcely a place that is not connected in some way

and his mighty men, but the old deities are still remembered, dwarfed

â€though round our seagirt shore they rise in crowdsâ€, had been â€by the
Celestial Muses glorifiedâ€ doubtless seemed true to his own generation.
hanks to the scholars who have unveiled the ancient Gaelic and ritish
mythologies, it need not be so for ours. n udgate ill, as well as on
many less famous eminences, once stood the temple of the ritish eus. A
mountain not far from ettwsyCoed was the ritish lympus, the court
and palace of our ancient gods.

It may well be doubted, however, whether Wordsworthâ€s contemporaries
would have welcomed the mythology which was their own by right of birth

had long been secretly leavening nglish ideas and ideals, none the less
potently because disguised under forms which could be readily
appreciated. opular fancy had rehabilitated the old gods, long banned
by the priestsâ€ bell, book, and candle, under various disguises. hey

fabulous past anterior to ulius Caesarâ€”such were ing ud, founder of

filling parts in old drama. hey still lived on as longdead saints of
the early churches of Ireland and ritain, whose wonderful attributes

another, and a yet more potent, way. Myths of Arthur and his cycle of
gods passed into the hands of the orman storytellers, to reappear as
romances of ing Arthur and his nights of the able ound. hus spread

his diverse influence of Celtic mythology upon nglish poetry and

bore the wounded Arthur to the peaceful valley. â€here was little

ir uon of ordeaux. It is the dreamy forest of Arden. In an older
mythology, it was the realm of a ing of hadows, the country of Gwyn ap
udd, who rode as ir Guyon in the â€airie ueeneâ€â€”

           â€And knighthood took of good ir uonâ€s hand,
            When with ing beron he came to airylandâ€.â€

o trace Welsh and Irish kings and saints and hermits back to â€the
elemental powers of earth and fire, and the spirits that haunted the
woods and streamsâ€ of Celtic imagination, and to disclose primitive

Arthurâ€s nightsâ€ will necessarily fall within the scope of this volume.
ut meanwhile the reader will probably be asking what evidence there is

he answer to his uestion will be found in the next chapter.

We may begin by asserting with confidence that Mr. lton has touched
upon a part only of the material on which we may draw, to reconstruct
the ancient ritish mythology. uckily, we are not wholly dependent upon
the difficult tasks of resolving the fabled deeds of apocryphal Irish
and ritish kings who reigned earlier than t. atrick or before ulius
Caesar into their original form of Celtic myths, of sifting the
attributes and miracles of doubtfully historical saints, or of
separating the primitive pagan elements in the legends of Arthur and his
nights from the embellishments added by the romancewriters. We have,
in addition to theseâ€”which we may for the present put upon one side as
secondaryâ€”sources, a mass of genuine early writings which, though
postChristian in the form in which they now exist, none the less
descend from the preceding pagan age. hese are contained in vellum and
parchment manuscripts long preserved from destruction in mansions and
monasteries in Ireland, cotland, and Wales, and only during the last
century brought to light, copied, and translated by the patient labours
of scholars who have grappled with the longobsolete dialects in which
they were transcribed.

Many of these volumes are curious miscellanies. sually the one book of
a great house or monastic community, everything was copied into it that
the scholar of the family or brotherhood thought to be best worth
preserving. ence they contain matter of the most diverse kind. here
are translations of portions of the ible and of the classics, and of
such then popular books as Geoffrey of Monmouthâ€s and enniusâ€ istories
of ritain lives of famous saints, together with works attributed to
them poems and romances of which, under a thin disguise, the old Gaelic
and ritish gods are the heroes together with treatises on all the
subects then studiedâ€”grammar, prosody, law, history, geography,
chronology, and the genealogies of important chiefs.

he maority of these documents were put together during a period which,
roughly speaking, lasted from the beginning of the twelfth century to
the end of the sixteenth. In Ireland, in Wales, and, apparently, also in
cotland, it was a time of literary revival after the turmoils of the
previous epoch. In Ireland, the orsemen, after long ravaging, had
settled peacefully down, while in Wales, the orman Conuest had
rendered the country for the first time comparatively uiet. he
scattered remains of history, lay and ecclesiastical, of science, and of
legend were gathered together.

f the Irish manuscripts, the earliest, and, for our purposes, the most
important, on account of the great store of ancient Gaelic mythology
which, in spite of its dilapidated condition, it still contains, is in
the possession of the oyal Irish Academy. nluckily, it is reduced to a
fragment of one hundred and thirtyeight pages, but this remnant
preserves a large number of romances relating to the old gods and heroes
of Ireland. Among other things, it contains a complete account of the
epical saga called the in  Chuailgn, the â€aiding of the Cattle
of Cooleyâ€, in which the hero, Cuchulainn, performed his greatest feats.
his manuscript is called the ook of the un Cow, from the tradition
that it was copied from an earlier book written upon the skin of a
favourite animal belonging to aint Ciaran, who lived in the seventh
century. An entry upon one of its pages reveals the name of its scribe,
one Maelmuiri, whom we know to have been killed by robbers in the church
of Clonmacnois in the year 0.

ar more voluminous, and but little less ancient, is the ook of
einster, said to have been compiled in the early part of the twelfth
century by inn mac Gorman, ishop of ildare. his also contains an
account of Cuchulainnâ€s mighty deeds which supplements the older version
in the ook of the un Cow. f somewhat less importance from the point
of view of the student of Gaelic mythology come the ook of allymote
and the Yellow ook of ecan, belonging to the end of the fourteenth
century, and the ooks of ecan and of ismore, both attributed to the
fifteenth. esides these six great collections, there survive many other
manuscripts which also contain ancient mythical lore. In one of these,
dating from the fifteenth century, is to be found the story of the
attle of Moytura, fought between the gods of Ireland and their enemies,
the omors, or demons of the deep sea.

he cottish manuscripts, preserved in the Advocatesâ€ ibrary at
dinburgh, date back in some cases as far as the fourteenth century,
though the maority of them belong to the fifteenth and sixteenth. hey
corroborate the Irish documents, add to the Cuchulainn saga, and make a
more special subect of the other heroic cycle, that which relates the
not less wonderful deeds of inn, ssian, and the enians. hey also
contain stories of other characters, who, more ancient than either inn
or Cuchulainn, are the uatha  anann, the godtribe of the ancient
Gaels.

he Welsh documents cover about the same period as the Irish and the
cottish. our of these stand out from the rest, as most important. he
oldest is the lack ook of Caermarthen, which dates from the third
uarter of the twelfth century the ook of Aneurin, which was written
late in the thirteenth the ook of aliesin, assigned to the
fourteenth and the ed ook of ergest, compiled by various persons
during that century and the one following it. he first three of these
â€our Ancient ooks of Walesâ€ are small in size, and contain poems
attributed to the great traditional bards of the sixth century, Myrddin,
aliesin, and Aneurin. he lastâ€”the ed ook of ergestâ€”is far larger.
In it are to be found Welsh translations of the ritish Chronicles the
oftmentioned riads, verses celebrating famous traditionary persons or
things ancient poems attributed to lywarch n and, of priceless
value to any study of our subect, the socalled Mabinogion, stories in
which large portions of the old ritish mythology are worked up into
romantic form.

he whole bulk, therefore, of the native literature bearing upon the
mythology of the ritish Islands may be attributed to a period which
lasted from the beginning of the twelfth century to the end of the
sixteenth. ut even the commencement of this era will no doubt seem far
too late a day to allow authenticity to matter which ought to have
vastly preceded it. he date, however, merely marks the final redaction
of the contents of the manuscripts into the form in which they now
exist, without bearing at all upon the time of their authorship.
Avowedly copies of ancient poems and tales from much older manuscripts,
the present books no more fix the period of the original composition of
their contents than the presence of a portion of the Canterbury ales
in a modern anthology of nglish poetry would assign Chaucer to the
present year of grace.

his may be proved both directly and inferentially. In some
instancesâ€”as in that of an elegy upon aint Columba in the ook of the
un Cowâ€”the dates of authorship are actually given. In others, we may
depend upon evidence which, if not uite so absolute, is nearly as
convincing. ven where the writer does not state that he is copying from
older manuscripts, it is obvious that this must have been the case, from
the glosses in his version. he scribes of the earlier Gaelic
manuscripts very often found, in the documents from which they
themselves were copying, words so archaic as to be unintelligible to the
readers of their own period. o render them comprehensible, they were
obliged to insert marginal notes which explained these obsolete words by
reference to other manuscripts more ancient still. ften the medival
copyists have ignorantly moved these notes from the margin into the
text, where they remain, like philological fossils, to give evidence of
previous forms of life. he documents from which they were taken have
perished, leaving the medival copies as their sole record. In the Welsh
Mabinogion the same process is apparent. eculiarities in the existing
manuscripts show plainly enough that they must have been copied from
some more archaic text. esides this, they are, as they at present
stand, obviously made up of earlier tales pieced together. Almost as
clearly as the Gaelic manuscripts, the Welsh point us back to older and
more primitive forms.

he ancient legends of the Gael and the riton are thus shown to have
been no mere inventions of scholarly monks in the Middle Ages. We have
now to trace, if possible, the date, not necessarily of their first
appearance on menâ€s lips, but of their first redaction into writing in
approximately the form in which we have them now.

Circumstantial evidence can be adduced to prove that the most important
portions both of Gaelic and ritish early literature can be safely
relegated to a period of several centuries prior to their nowexisting
record. ur earliest version of the episode of the in  Chuailgn,
which is the nucleus and centre of the ancient Gaelic heroic cycle of
which Cuchulainn, fortissimus heros cotorum, is the principal figure,
is found in the twelfthcentury ook of the un Cow. ut legend tells us
that at the beginning of the seventh century the aga had not only been
composed, but had actually become so obsolete as to have been forgotten
by the bards. heir leader, one enchan orpeist, a historical
character, and chief bard of Ireland at that time, obtained permission
from the aints to call ergus, Cuchulainnâ€s contemporary, and a chief
actor in the â€aidâ€, from the dead, and received from the resurrected
hero a true and full version. his tradition, dealing with a real
personage, surely shows that the story of the in was known before
the time of enchan, and probably preserves the fact, either that his
version of Cuchulainnâ€s famous deeds became the accepted one, or that he
was the first to reduce it to writing. An eually suggestive
consideration approximately fixes for us the earliest redaction of the
Welsh mythological prose tales called the â€Mabinogionâ€, or, more
correctly speaking, the â€our ranches of the Mabinogiâ€. In none of
these is there the slightest mention, or apparently the least knowledge,
of Arthur, around whom and whose supposed contemporaries centres the
mass of ritish legend as it was transmitted by the Welsh to the
ormans. hese mysterious mythological records must in all probability,
therefore, antedate the Arthurian cycle of myth, which was already being
put into form in the sixth century. n the other hand, the characters of
the â€our ranchesâ€ are mentioned without commentâ€”as though they were
personages with whom no one could fail to be familiarâ€”in the supposed
sixthcentury poems contained in those â€our Ancient ooks of Walesâ€ in
which are found the first meagre references to the ritish hero.

uch considerations as these throw back, with reasonable certainty, the
existence of the Irish and Welsh poems and prose tales, in something
like their present shape, to a period antedating the seventh century.

ut this, again, means only that the myths, traditions, and legends were
current at that to us early, but to them, in their actual substance,
late date, in literary form. A mythology must always be far older than
the oldest verses and stories that celebrate it. laborate poems and
sagas are not made in a day, or in a year. he legends of the Gaelic and
ritish gods and heroes could not have sprung, like Athena from the head
of eus, fullborn out of some poetâ€s brain. he bard who first put them
into artistic shape was setting down the primitive traditions of his
race. We may therefore venture to describe them as not of the twelfth
century or of the seventh, but as of a prehistoric and immemorial
antiuity.

Internal evidence bears this out. An examination of both the Gaelic and
ritish legendary romances shows, under embellishing details added by
later hands, an inner core of primeval thought which brings them into
line with the similar ideas of other races in the earliest stage of
culture. heir â€local colourâ€ may be that of their last â€editorâ€, but
their â€plotsâ€ are premedival, preChristian, prehistoric. he
characters of early Gaelic legend belong to the same stamp of
imagination that created lympian and itan, sir and tun. We must go
far to the back of civilized thought to find parallels to such a story
as that in which the ritish sungod, struck by a rival in love with a
poisoned spear, is turned into an eagle, from whose wound great pieces
of carrion are continually failing.

his aspect of the Celtic literary records was clearly seen, and
elouently expressed, by Matthew Arnold in his tudy of Celtic
iterature. e was referring to the Welsh side, but his image holds
good eually for the Gaelic. â€he first thing that strikes oneâ€, he
says, â€in reading the Mabinogion is how evidently the medival
storyteller is pillaging an antiuity of which he does not fully
possess the secret he is like a peasant building his hut on the site of
alicarnassus or phesus he builds, but what he builds is full of
materials of which he knows not the history, or knows by a glimmering
tradition merely stones â€not of this buildingâ€, but of an older
architecture, greater, cunninger, more maestical.â€ is heroes â€are no
medival personages they belong to an older, pagan, mythological
worldâ€. o, too, with the figures, however euhemerized, of the three
great Gaelic cycles that of the uatha  anann, of the eroes of
lster, of inn and the enians. heir divinity outshines their
humanity through their masks may be seen the faces of gods.

Yet, gods as they are, they had taken on the semblance of mortality by
the time their histories were fixed in the form in which we have them
now. heir earliest records, if those could be restored to us, would
doubtless show them eternal and undying, changing their shapes at will,
but not passing away. ut the postChristian copyists, whether Irish or
Welsh, would not countenance this. ence we have the singular paradox of
the deaths of Immortals. here is hardly one of the figures of either
the Gaelic or the ritish antheon whose demise is not somewhere
recorded. sually they fell in the unceasing battles between the
divinities of darkness and of light. heir deaths in earlier cycles of
myth, however, do not preclude their appearance in later ones. nly,
indeed, with the closing of the lips of the last mortal who preserved
his tradition can the life of a god be truly said to end.

ut, before proceeding to recount the myths of the â€Ancient ritonsâ€, it
will be well to decide what people, exactly, we mean by that loose but
convenient phrase. We have, all of us, vague ideas of Ancient ritons,
recollected, doubtless, from our schoolbooks. here we saw their
pictures as, painted with woad, they paddled coracles, or drove scythed
chariots through legions of astonished omans. heir ruids,
whitebearded and wearing long, white robes, cut the mistletoe with a
golden sickle at the time of the full moon, or, less innocently
employed, made bonfires of human beings shut up in gigantic figures of
wickerwork.

uch picturesue details were little short of the sumtotal, not only of
our own knowledge of the subect, but also of that of our teachers.
ractically all their information concerning the ancient inhabitants of
ritain was taken from the Commentaries of ulius Caesar. o far as it
went, it was no doubt correct but it did not go far. Caesarâ€s interest
in our ritish ancestors was that of a general who was his own
warcorrespondent rather than that of an exhaustive and painstaking
scientist. It has been reserved for modern archologists, philologists,
and ethnologists to give us a fuller account of the Ancient ritons.

he inhabitants of our islands previous to the oman invasion are
generally described as â€Celtsâ€. ut they must have been largely a mixed
race and the people with whom they mingled must have modified to
someâ€”and perhaps to a largeâ€”extent their physiue, their customs, and
their language.

peculation has run somewhat wild over the uestion of the composition
of the arly ritons. ut out of the clash of rival theories there
emerges oneâ€”and one onlyâ€”which may be considered as scientifically
established. We have certain proof of two distinct human stocks in the
ritish Islands at the time of the oman Conuest and so great an
authority as rofessor uxley has given his opinion that there is no
evidence of any others.

he earliest of these two races would seem to have inhabited our islands
from the most ancient times, and may, for our purpose, be described as
aboriginal. It was the people that built the â€long barrowsâ€ and which
is variously called by ethnologists the Iberian, Mediterranean, erber,
asue, ilurian, or uskarian race. In physiue it was short, swarthy,
darkhaired, darkeyed, and longskulled its language belonged to the
class called â€amiticâ€, the surviving types of which are found among the
Gallas, Abyssinians, erbers, and other orth African tribes and it
seems to have come originally from some part either of astern,
orthern, or Central Africa. preading thence, it was probably the first
people to inhabit the alley of the ile, and it sent offshoots into
yria and Asia Minor. he earliest ellenes found it in Greece under the
name of â€elasgoiâ€ the earliest atins in Italy, as the â€truscansâ€
and the ebrews in alestine, as the â€ittitesâ€. It spread northward
through urope as far as the altic, and westward, along the Atlas

agricultural rather than a pastoral people, still in the tone Age,
dwelling in totemistic tribes on hills whose summits it fortified

â€terrace systemâ€, and having a primitive culture which ethnologists
think to have much resembled that of the present hilltribes of outhern
India. It held our islands till the coming of the Celts, who fought
with the aborigines, dispossessed them of the more fertile parts,
subugated them, even amalgamated with them, but certainly never
extirpated them. In the time of the omans they were still practically
independent in outh Wales. In Ireland they were long unconuered, and
are found as allies rather than serfs of the Gaels, ruling their own
provinces, and preserving their own customs and religion. or, in spite
of all the successive invasions of Great ritain and Ireland, are they
yet extinct, or so merged as to have lost their type, which is still the
predominant one in many parts of the west both of ritain and Ireland,
and is believed by some ethnologists to be generally upon the increase
all over ngland.

he second of the two races was the exact opposite to the first. It was
the tall, fair, lighthaired, blue or grayeyed, broadheaded people
called, popularly, the â€Celtsâ€, who belonged in speech to the â€Aryanâ€
family, their language finding its affinities in atin, Greek, eutonic,
lavic, the end of Ancient ersia, and the anscrit of Ancient India.
Its original home was probably somewhere in Central urope, along the
course of the upper anube, or in the region of the Alps. he â€round

differ in shape from the â€long barrowsâ€ of the earlier race. It was in a
higher stage of culture than the â€Iberiansâ€, and introduced into ritain
bronze and silver, and, perhaps, some of the more lately domesticated
animals.

oth Iberians and Celts were divided into numerous tribes, but there is
nothing to show that there was any great diversity among the former. It
is otherwise with the Celts, who were separated into two main branches
which came over at different times. he earliest were the Goidels, or
Gaels the second, the rythons, or ritons. etween these two branches
there was not only a dialectical, but probably, also, a considerable
physical difference. ome anthropologists even postulate a different
shape of skull. Without necessarily admitting this, there is reason to
suppose a difference of build and of colour of hair. With regard to
this, we have the evidence of atin writersâ€”of acitus,2 who tells us

rofessor hys also puts forward evidence to show that the Goidels and
the rythons had already separated before they first left Gaul for our

third by those who called themselves Celtae, but were termed Galli by
the omans and that they all differed from one another in language,

the Auitani, being nonCeltic and nonAryan, part of the great
amiticspeaking Iberian stock. he Celtae, with their Goidelic
dialect of Celtic, which survives today in the Gaelic languages of
Ireland, cotland, and the Isle of Man, were the first to come over to
ritain, pushed forward, probably, by the elgae, who, Caesar tells us,
were the bravest of the Gauls. ere they conuered the native
Iberians, driving them out of the fertile parts into the rugged

Iberians. hus harried, the Goidels probably combined with the Iberians
against what was now the common foe, and became to a large degree
amalgamated with them. he result was that during the oman domination
the ritish Islands were roughly divided with regard to race as follows
he rythons, or second Celtic race, held all ritain south of the
weed, with the exception of the extreme west, while the first Celtic
race, the Goidelic, had most of Ireland, as well as the Isle of Man,
Cumberland, the West ighlands, Cornwall, evon, and orth Wales. orth
of the Grampians lived the icts, who were probably more or less
Goidelicized Iberians, the aboriginal race also holding out, unmixed, in
outh Wales and parts of Ireland.

It is now time to decide what, for the purposes of this book, it will be
best to call the two different branches of the Celts, and their
languages. With such familiar terms as â€Gaelâ€ and â€ritonâ€, â€Gaelicâ€ and
â€ritishâ€, ready to our hands, it seems pedantic to insist upon the more
technical â€Goidelâ€ and â€rythonâ€, â€Goidelicâ€ and â€rythonicâ€. he
difficulty is that the words â€Gaelâ€ and â€Gaelicâ€ have been so long
popularly used to designate only the modern â€Goidelsâ€ of cotland and
their language, that they may create confusion when also applied to the
people and languages of Ireland and the Isle of Man. imilarly, the
words â€ritonâ€ and â€ritishâ€ have come to mean, at the present day, the
people of the whole of the ritish Islands, though they at first only
signified the inhabitants of ngland, Central Wales, the owlands of
cotland, and the rythonic colony in rittany. owever, the words
â€Goidelâ€ and â€rythonâ€, with their derivatives, are so clumsy that it
will probably prove best to use the neater terms. In this volume,
therefore, the â€Goidelsâ€ of Ireland, cotland, and the Isle of Man are
our â€Gaelsâ€ and the â€rythonsâ€ of ngland and Wales are our â€ritonsâ€.

We get the earliest accounts of the life of the inhabitants of the
ritish Islands from two sources. he first is a foreign one, that of
the atin writers. ut the omans only really knew the outhern ritons,
whom they describe as similar in physiue and customs to the Continental
Gauls, with whom, indeed, they considered them to be identical. At
the time they wrote, colonies of elgae were still settling upon the
coasts of ritain opposite to Gaul.20 oman information grew scantier
as it approached the Wall, and of the orthern tribes they seem to have
had only such knowledge as they gathered through occasional warfare with
them. hey describe them as entirely barbarous, naked and tattooed,
living by the chase alone, without towns, houses, or fields, without
government or family life, and regarding iron as an ornament of value,
as other, more civilized peoples regarded gold.2 As for Ireland, it
never came under their direct observation, and we are entirely dependent
upon its native writers for information as to the manners and customs of
the Gaels. It may be considered convincing proof of the authenticity of
the descriptions of life contained in the ancient Gaelic manuscripts
that they corroborate so completely the observations of the atin
writers upon the ritons and Gauls. eading the two side by side, we may
largely reconstruct the common civilization of the Celts.

oughly speaking, one may compare it with the civilization of the
Greeks, as described by omer.22 oth peoples were in the tribal and
pastoral stage of culture, in which the chiefs are the great
cattleowners round whom their less wealthy fellows gather. oth wear
much the same attire, use the same kind of weapons, and fight in the
same mannerâ€”from the warchariot, a vehicle already obsolete even in
Ireland by the first century of the Christian era. attles are fought
singlehanded between chiefs, the illarmed common people contributing
little to their result, and less to their history. uch chiefs are said
to be divinely descendedâ€”sons, even, of the immortal gods. heir
tremendous feats are sung by the bards, who, like the omeric poets,
were privileged persons, inferior only to the warlord. Ancient Greek
and Ancient Celt had very much the same conceptions of life, both as
regards this world and the next.

We may gather much detailed information of the early inhabitants of the
ritish Islands from our various authorities.2 heir clothes, which
consisted, according to the atin writers, of a blouse with sleeves,
trousers fitting closely round the ankles, and a shawl or cloak,
fastened at the shoulder with a brooch, were made either of thick felt
or of woven cloth dyed with various brilliant colours. he writer

â€as though they had been sprinkled with flowersâ€. hey were, in fact,
like â€tartansâ€, and we may believe arro, who tells us that they â€made a
gaudy showâ€. he men alone seem to have worn hats, which were of soft

their abundant hair forward into a thick mass, and dyeing it red with a
soap made of goatâ€s fat and beech ashes, until they looked says

human beings than wild men of the woods. oth sexes were fond of
ornaments, which took the form of gold bracelets, rings, pins, and
brooches, and of beads of amber, glass, and et. heir knives, daggers,
spearheads, axes, and swords were made of bronze or iron their shields

Culloden and they seem also to have had a kind of lasso to which a
hammershaped ball was attached, and which they used as the Gauchos of

the wooden wheels being armed with sickles of bronze. hese were drawn
either by two or four horses, and were large enough to hold several
persons in each. tanding in these, they rushed along the enemyâ€s lines,
hurling darts, and driving the scythes against all who came within
reach. he omans were much impressed by the skill of the drivers, who
â€could check their horses at full speed on a steep incline, and turn
them in an instant, and could run along the pole, and stand on the yoke,
and then get back into their chariots again without a momentâ€s
delayâ€.2

With these accounts of the oman writers we may compare the picture of
the Gaelic hero, Cuchulainn, as the ancient Irish writers describe him
dressed and armed for battle. Glorified by the bard, he yet wears
essentially the same costume and euipment which the classic historians
and geographers described more soberly. â€is gorgeous raiment that he

plies and fringed, with a long pin of white silver, goldenchased and

strung.â€ e carried â€a trusty special shield, in hue dark crimson, and

lengthy spear together with a keen, aggressionboding avelin, fitted
with hurling thong, with rivets of white bronze.â€2 Another passage of
Gaelic saga describes his chariot. It was made of fine wood, with

white silver, with mountings of white bronze. he yellow reins were
plaited, and the shafts were as hard and straight as swordblades.2

In like manner the ancient Irish writers have made glorious the halls
and fortresses of their mythical kings. ike the palaces of riam, of
Menelaus, and of dysseus, they gleam with gold and gems. Conchobar,2
the legendary ing of lster in its golden age, had three such â€housesâ€
at main Macha. f the one called the â€ed ranchâ€, we are told that it
contained nine compartments of red yew, partitioned by walls of bronze,
all grouped around the kingâ€s private chamber, which had a ceiling of
silver, and bronze pillars adorned with gold and carbuncles.2 ut the
far less magnificent accounts of the atin writers have, no doubt, more
truth in them than such lavish pictures. hey described the ritons they
knew as living in villages of beehive huts, roofed with fern or thatch,
from which, at the approach of an enemy, they retired to the local
dn. his, so far from being elaborate, merely consisted of a round or
oval space fenced in with palisades and earthworks, and situated either
upon the top of a hill or in the midst of a not easily traversable

Georgeâ€s ill, in urreyâ€”and it is probable that, in spite of the Celtic
praisers of past days, the â€palacesâ€ of main Macha and of ara were

he Celtic customs were, like the omeric, those of the primitive world.
All land though it may have theoretically belonged to the chief was
cultivated in common. his community of possessions is stated by
Caesar0 to have extended to their wives but the imputation cannot be
said to have been proved. n the contrary, in the stories of both
branches of the Celtic race, women seem to have taken a higher place in
menâ€s estimation, and to have enoyed far more personal liberty, than
among the omeric Greeks. he idea may have arisen from a
misunderstanding of some of the curious Celtic customs. escent seems to
have been traced through the maternal rather than through the paternal
line, a very unAryan procedure which some believe to have been borrowed
from another race. he parental relation was still further lessened by
the custom of sending children to be brought up outside the family in
which they were born, so that they had fosterparents to whom they were
as much, or even more, attached than to their natural ones.

heir political state, mirroring their family life, was not less
primitive. here was no central tribunal. isputes were settled within
the families in which they occurred, while, in the case of graver
inuries, the inured party or his nearest relation could kill the
culprit or exact a fine from him. As families increased in number, they
became petty tribes, often at war with one another. A defeated tribe had
to recognize the sovereignty of the head man of the conuering tribe,
and a succession of such victories exalted him into the position of a
chief of his district. ut even then, though his decision was the whole
of the law, he was little more than the mouthpiece of public opinion.

he ancient inhabitants of ritainâ€”the Gaelic and ritish Celtsâ€”have
been already described as forming a branch of what are roughly called
the â€Aryansâ€. his name has, however, little reference to race, and
really signifies the speakers of a group of languages which can be all
shown to be connected, and to descend remotely from a single sourceâ€”a
hypothetical mothertongue spoken by a hypothetical people which we term
â€Aryanâ€, or, more correctly, â€Indouropeanâ€. his primeval speech,
evolved, probably, upon some part of the great plain which stretches
from the mountains of Central urope to the mountains of Central Asia,
has spread, superseding, or amalgamating with the tongues of other
races, until branches of it are spoken over almost the whole of urope
and a great portion of Asia. All the various atin, Greek, lavic,
eutonic, and Celtic languages are â€Aryanâ€, as well as ersian and other
Asiatic dialects derived from the ancient â€endâ€, and the numerous
Indian languages which trace their origin to anscrit.

ot very long ago, it was supposed that this common descent of language
involved a common descent of blood. A real brotherhood was
enthusiastically claimed for all the principal uropean nations, who
were also invited to recognize indus and ersians as their longlost
cousins. ince then, it has been conceded that, while the Aryan speech
survived, though greatly modified, the Aryan blood might well have
disappeared, diluted beyond recognition by crossing with the other races
whom the Aryans conuered, or among whom they more or less peacefully
settled. As a matter of fact, there are no uropean nationsâ€”perhaps no
people at all except a few remote savage tribesâ€”which are not made up of
the most diverse elements. Aryan and nonAryan long ago blended
inextricably, to form by their fusion new peoples.

ut, ust as the Aryan speech influenced the new languages, and the
Aryan customs the new civilizations, so we can still discern in the
religions of the Aryanspeaking nations similar ideas and expressions
pointing to an original source of mythological conceptions. ence,
whether we investigate the mythology of the indus, the Greeks, the
eutons, or the Celts, we find the same mythological groundwork. In
each, we see the powers of nature personified, and endowed with human
form and attributes, though bearing, with few exceptions, different
names. ike the edic brahmans, the Greek and atin poets, and the orse
scalds, the Celtic bardsâ€”whether Gaels or ritonsâ€”imagined the sky, the
sun, the moon, the earth, the sea, and the dark underworld, as well as
the mountains, the streams and the woods, to be ruled by beings like
their own chiefs, but infinitely more powerful every passion, as War
and ove, and every art, as oetry and mithcraft, had its divine
founder, teacher, and exponent and of all these deities and their
imagined children, they wove the poetical and allegorical romances which
form the subect of the present volume.

ike other nations, too, whether Aryan or nonAryan, the Celts had,
besides their mythology, a religion. It is not enough to tell tales of
shadowy gods they must be made visible by sculpture, housed in groves
or temples, served with ritual, and propitiated with sacrifices, if one
is to hope for their favours. very cult must have its priests living by
the altar.

he priests of the Celts are wellknown to us by name as the â€ruidsâ€â€”a
word derived from a root  which signifies a tree, and especially the
oak, in several Aryan languages. his is generallyâ€”though not by all
scholarsâ€”taken as proving that they paid an especial veneration to the
king of trees. It is true that the mistletoeâ€”that strange parasite upon
the oakâ€”was prominent among their â€herbs of powerâ€, and played a part in
their ritual2 but this is eually true of other Aryan nations. y
the orse it was held sacred to the god alder, while the omans
believed it to be the â€golden boughâ€ that gave access to ades.

he accounts both of the atin and Gaelic writers give us a fairly
complete idea of the nature of the ruids, and especially of the high
estimation in which they were held. hey were at once the priests, the
physicians, the wizards, the diviners, the theologians, the scientists,
and the historians of their tribes. All spiritual power and all human
knowledge were vested in them, and they ranked second only to the kings
and chiefs. hey were freed from all contribution to the tate, whether
by tribute or service in war, so that they might the better apply
themselves to their divine offices. heir decisions were absolutely
final, and those who disobeyed them were laid under a terrible
excommunication or â€boycottâ€. Classic writers tell us how they
lorded it in Gaul, where, no doubt, they borrowed splendour by imitating
their more civilized neighbours. Men of the highest rank were proud to
cast aside the insignia of mere mortal honour to oin the company of
those who claimed to be the direct mediators with the skygod and the
thundergod, and who must have resembled the ecclesiastics of medival
urope in the days of their greatest power, combining, like them,
spiritual and temporal dignities, and possessing the highest culture of
their age. Yet it was not among these ruids of Gaul, with their
splendid temples and vestments and their elaborate rituals, that the
metropolis of ruidism was to be sought. We learn from Caesar that the
Gallic ruids believed their religion to have come to them, originally,
from ritain, and that it was their practice to send their â€theological
studentsâ€ across the Channel to learn its doctrines at their purest
source. o trace a cult backwards is often to take a retrograde
course in culture, and it was no doubt in ritainâ€”which liny the lder
tells us â€might have taught magic to ersiaâ€â€”that the sufficiently
primitive and savage rites of the ruids of Gaul were preserved in their
still more savage and primitive forms. It is curious corroboration of
this alleged ritish origin of ruidism that the ancient Irish also
believed their ruidism to have come from the sister island. heir
heroes and seers are described as only gaining the highest knowledge by
travelling to Alba. owever this may be, we may take it as certain
that this ruidism was the accepted religion of the Celtic race.

Certain scholars look deeper for its origin, holding its dark
superstitions and savage rites to bear the stamp of lower minds than
those of the poetic and manly Celts. rofessor hys inclines to see
three forms of religion in the ritish Islands at the time of the oman

f the famous teaching of the ruids we know little, owing to their

It would appear probable that the Celtic tate worship was what is

he same great gods were, no doubt, adored by all the Celts, not only of

naturally held their own gods to be the stronger. till, it could not be
denied that the gods of the conuered were upon their own ground they

his raises the uestion of the exact nationality of the gods whose

f the two Celtic races that settled in our islands, it is the earlier,
the Gaels, that has best preserved its old mythology. It is true that we
have in few cases such detailed account of the Gaelic gods as we gain of
the ellenic deities from the Greek poets, of the Indian evas from the
ig eda, or of the orse sir from the ddas. Yet none the less may we
draw from the ancient Irish manuscripts uite enough information to
enable us to set forth their figures with some clearness. We find them,
as might have been anticipated, very much like the divine hierarchies of
other Aryan peoples.

We also find them separated into two opposing camps, a division common
to all the Aryan religions. ust as the lympians struggled with the
Giants, the sir fought the tuns, and the evas the Asuras, so there
is warfare in the Gaelic spiritual world between two superhuman hosts.
n one side are ranged the gods of day, light, life, fertility, wisdom,
and good on the other, the demons of night, darkness, death,
barrenness, and evil. he first were the great spirits symbolizing the
beneficial aspects of nature and the arts and intelligence of man the
second were the hostile powers thought to be behind such baneful
manifestations as storm and fog, drought and disease. he first are
ranged as a divine family round a goddess called anu, from whom they
took their wellknown name of uatha  anann, â€ribeâ€ or â€olk
of the Goddess anuâ€. he second owned allegiance to a female divinity
called omnu their king, Indech, is described as her son, and they are

the abyss or the deep sea, and the same idea is also expressed in
their betterknown name of â€omorsâ€, derived from two Gaelic words
meaning â€under seaâ€. he waste of water seems to have always
impressed the Celts with the sense of primeval ancientness it was
connected in their minds with vastness, darkness, and monstrous
birthsâ€”the very antithesis of all that was symbolized by the earth, the
sky, and the sun.

herefore the omors were held to be more ancient than the gods, before
whom they were, however, destined to fall in the end. ffspring of
â€Chaos and ld ightâ€, they were, for the most part, huge and deformed.

he people of the goddess anu were not the first divine inhabitants of
Ireland. thers had been before them, dwellers in â€the dark backward and

hese would seem to have been inevitable. Whatever gods ruled, they

consent to this, the uatha  anann offered them a fifth part of
Ireland, whichever province they might choose. hey agreed, and chose
Connaught, ever afterwards their especial home, and where, until the
middle of the seventeenth century, men were still found tracing their
descent from reng.

he whole story has a singularly historical, curiously unmythological
air about it, which contrasts strangely with the account of the other
battle of the same name which the uatha  anann waged afterwards with
the omors. he neighbourhood of Cong still preserves both relics and
traditions of the fight. pon the plain of â€outhern Moyturaâ€ as it is
called, to distinguish it from the â€orthern Moyturaâ€ of the second
battle are many circles and tumuli. hese circles are especially
numerous near the village itself and it is said that there were
formerly others, which have been used for making walls and dykes. arge
cairns of stones, too, are scattered over what was certainly once the
scene of a great battle.0 hese various prehistoric monuments each
have their stilltold story and ir William Wilde, as he relates in his
ough Corrib,0 was so impressed by the unexpected agreement
between the details of the legendary battle, as he read them in the
ancient manuscript, and the traditions still attaching to the mounds,
circles, and cairns, that he tells us he could not help coming to the
conclusion that the account was absolutely historical. Certainly the
coincidences are curious. is opinion was that the â€ir olgsâ€ were a
colony of elg, and that the â€uatha  anannâ€ were anes. ut the
people of the goddess anu are too obviously mythical to make it worth
while to seek any standingground for them in the world of reality. In
their superhuman attributes, they are uite different from the ir
olgs. In the epical cycle it is made as clear that the uatha  anann
are divine beings as it is that the ir olg, the ir omnann, and the
ir Gaillion stand on exactly the same footing as the men of lster.
ater history records by what Milesian kings and on what terms of
rackrent the three tribes were allowed settlements in other parts of
Ireland than their native Connaught. hey appear in ancient, medival,
and almost modern chronicles as the old race of the land. he truth
seems to be that the whole story of the war between the gods and the ir
olgs is an invention of comparatively late times. In the earliest
documents there is only one battle of Moytura, fought between the people
of the goddess anu and the omors. he idea of doubling it seems to
date from after the eleventh century0 and its inventor may very
well have used the legends concerning this battlefield, where two
unknown armies had fought in days gone by, in compiling his story. It
never belonged to the same genuine mythological stratum as the legend of
the original battle fought by the uatha  anann, the gods of the
Gaels, against the omors, the gods of the Iberians.

It was as a result of the loss of his hand in this battle with the ir

he preparations for this war are said to have lasted seven years. It

y this time the seven years of preparation had come to an end. A week

was uite otherwise with the people of the goddess anu. Weapons

he omors decided to send someone to discover the secret of these

together so adroitly that his bronze nails needed no hammering in. e

Whether the horses were domesticated ones buried with their riders, or
wild ones eaten at the funeral feasts, it would be hard to decide. he
history of the real event must have been long lost even at the early
date when its relics were pointed out as the records of a battle between
the gods and the giants of Gaelic myth.

f what adb had in mind when she uttered this prophecy we have no
record. ut it was true. he twilight of the Irish gods was at hand. A
new race was coming across the sea to dispute the ownership of Ireland
with the people of the goddess anu. And these newcomers were not
divinities like themselves, but men like ourselves, ancestors of the
Gaels.

his story of the conuest of the gods by mortalsâ€”which seems such a
strange one to usâ€”is typically Celtic. he Gaelic mythology is the only
one which has preserved it in any detail but the doctrine would seem to
have been common at one time to all the Celts. It was, however, of less
shame to the gods than would otherwise have been for men were of as
divine descent as themselves. he dogma of the Celts was that men were
descended from the god of death, and first came from the and of the
ead to take possession of the present world. Caesar tells us, in
his too short account of the Gauls, that they believed themselves to be
sprung from is ater, the god of the underworld.2 In the Gaelic
mythology is ater was called il, a name which has for root the
syllable bel, meaning â€to dieâ€. he god eli in ritish mythology was
no doubt the same person, while the same idea is expressed by the same
root in the name of alor, the terrible omor whose glance was
death.

he postChristian Irish chroniclers, seeking to reconcile Christian
teachings with the still vital pagan mythology by changing the gods into
ancient kings and incorporating them into the annals of the country,
with appropriate dates, also disposed of the genuine early doctrine by
substituting pain for ades, and giving a highlyfanciful account of
the origin and wanderings of their ancestors. o use a ibernicism,
appropriate in this connection, the first Irishman was a cythian called
enius arsa. eprived of his own throne, he had settled in gypt, where
his son iul married a daughter of the reigning haraoh. er name was
cta, and she had a son called Goidel, whose greatgrandson was named
ber cot, the whole genealogy being probably invented to explain the
origin of the three names by which the Gaels called themselvesâ€”inn,
cot, and Goidel. enius and his family and clan were turned out of
gypt for refusing to oin in the persecution of the children of Israel,
and soourned in Africa for fortytwo years. heir wanderings took them
to â€the altars of the hilistines, by the ake of siersâ€ then, passing
between usicada and the hilly country of yria, they travelled through
Mauretania as far as the illars of ercules and thence landed in
pain, where they lived many years, greatly increasing and multiplying.
he same route is given by the twelfthcentury ritish historian,
Geoffrey of Monmouth, as that taken by rutus and the roans when they
came to colonize ritain. Its only connection with any kind of fact
is that it corresponds fairly well with what ethnologists consider must
have been the westward line of migration taken, not, curiously enough,
by the Aryan Celts, but by the preAryan Iberians.

It is sufficient for us to find the first men in pain, remembering that
â€painâ€ stood for the Celtic ades, or lysium. In this country regon,

Amergin was the first to land. lanting his right foot on Irish soil, he

he Welsh bard aliesin sings in the same strain as the druid Amergin

who, while still in a state of semibarbarism, saw, with some of the

With ber and remon, sons of Mil, and conuerors of the gods, begins a

great mythical king who, in almost all national histories, closes the
strictly mythological age, and inaugurates a new era of less obviously
divine, if hardly less apocryphal characters.

In spite, however, of the worship of the uatha  anann instituted by

hearing of the death of Christ.0

ut this is a very transparent monkish interpolation into the original
story. A uite different view is taken by most modern scholars, who

Cuchulainn may have been the name of a real Gaelic warrior, however
suspiciously he may now resemble the sungod, who is said to have been
his father. ing Conchobar may have been the real chief of a tribe of
Irish Celts before he became an adumbration of the Gaelic skygod. It is
the same problem that confronts us in dealing with the heroic legends of

demigods, or men et us call them all alikeâ€”whether they be Greek or

the older gods who were greater than they were.

We are stretching no point in calling them demigods, for they were
goddescended.2 Cuchulainn, the greatest hero of the lster cycle,

â€errestrialâ€ they may have been in form, but their acts were
superhuman. Indeed, compared with the more modest exploits of the heroes

their tens, these ltonians despatched their hundreds. hey came home

they sat down to meat, they devoured whole oxen, and drank their mead
from vats. With one stroke of their favourite swords they beheaded hills
for sport. he gods themselves hardly did more, and it is easy to
understand that in those old days not only might the sons of gods look
upon the daughters of men and find them fair, but immortal women also
need not be too proud to form passing alliances with mortal men.

ome of the older deities seem to have already passed out of memory at

he heroic age of Ireland was not, however, the mere orgy of battle
which one might assume from the previous chapter. It had room for its

he epoch of main Macha is followed in the annals of ancient Ireland by
a succession of monarchs who, though doubtless as mythical as ing
Conchobar and his court, seem to grow gradually more human. heir line
lasts for about two centuries, culminating in a dynasty with which
legend has occupied itself more than with its immediate predecessors.
his is the one which began, according to the annalists, in A.. ,
with the famous Conn â€the undredighterâ€, and, passing down to the
reign of his even more famous grandson, Cormac â€the Magnificentâ€, is
connected with the third Gaelic cycleâ€”that which relates the exploits of
inn and the enians. All these kings had their dealings with the
national gods. A story contained in a fifteenthcentury Irish
manuscript, and called â€he Championâ€s rophecyâ€,22 tells how ugh
appeared to Conn, enveloped him in a magic mist, led him away to an
enchanted palace, and there prophesied to him the number of his
descendants, the length of their reigns, and the manner of their deaths.
Another tradition relates how Connâ€s son, Connla, was wooed by a goddess
and borne away, like the ritish Arthur, in a boat of glass to the
arthly aradise beyond the sea.22 Yet another relates Connâ€s own
marriage with ecuma of the air kin, wife of that same abraid of the
uick and on word who, in another legend, married iban, the sister of
and, Cuchulainnâ€s fairy love. ecuma had been discovered in an intrigue
with Gaiar, a son of Manannn, and, banished from the â€and of romiseâ€,
crossed the sea that sunders mortals and immortals to offer her hand to
Conn. he Irish king wedded her, but evil came of the marriage. he grew
ealous of Connâ€s other son, Art, and insisted upon his banishment but
they agreed to play chess to decide which should go, and Art won. Art,
called â€the onelyâ€ because he had lost his brother Connla, was king
after Conn, but he is chiefly known to legend as the father of Cormac.

Many Irish stories occupy themselves with the fame of Cormac, who is
pictured as a great legislatorâ€”a Gaelic olomon. Certain traditions
credit him with having been the first to believe in a purer doctrine
than the Celtic polytheism, and even with having attempted to put down
druidism, in revenge for which a druid called Maelcen sent an evil
spirit who placed a salmonbone crossways in the kingâ€s throat, as he
sat at meat, and so compassed his death. Another class of stories,
however, make him an especial favourite with those same heathen deities.
Manannn son of r, was so anxious for his friendship that he decoyed
him into fairyland, and gave him a magic branch. It was of silver, and
bore golden apples, and, when it was shaken, it made such sweet music
that the wounded, the sick, and the sorrowful forgot their pains, and
were lulled into deep sleep. Cormac kept this treasure all his life
but, at his death, it returned into the hands of the gods.22

ing Cormac was a contemporary of inn mac Coul22, whom he appointed
head of the ianna22 irinn, more generally known as the â€eniansâ€.
Around inn and his men have gathered a cycle of legends which were
eually popular with the Gaels of both cotland and Ireland. We read of
their exploits in stories and poems preserved in the earliest Irish
manuscripts, while among the peasantry both of Ireland and of the West
ighlands their names and the stories connected with them are still
current lore. pon some of these floating traditions, as preserved in
folk ballads, Macherson founded his factitious ssian, and the
collection of them from the lips of living men still affords plenty of
employment to Gaelic students.

ow far inn and his followers may have been historical personages it is
impossible to say. he Irish people themselves have always held that the
enians were a kind of native militia, and that inn was their general.
he early historical writers of Ireland supported this view. he
chronicler ighernach, who died in 0, believed in him, and the
â€Annals of the our Mastersâ€, compiled between the years 2 and 
from older chronicles, while they ignore ing Conchobar and his ed
ranch Champions as unworthy of the serious consideration of historians,
treat inn as a real person whose death took place in 2 A.. ven so
great a modern scholar as ugene â€Curry declared in the clearest
language that inn, so far from being â€a merely imaginary or mythical
characterâ€, was â€an undoubtedly historical personage and that he
existed about the time at which his appearance is recorded in the Annals
is as certain as that ulius Caesar lived and ruled at the time stated
on the authority of the oman historiansâ€.22

he opinion of more recent Celtic scholars, however, is opposed to this
view. innâ€s pedigree, preserved in the ook of einster, may seem at
first to give some support to the theory of his real existence, but, on
more careful examination of it, his own name and that of his father
eually bewray him. inn or ionn, meaning â€fairâ€, is the name of one of
the mythical ancestors of the Gaels, while his fatherâ€s name,
Cumhal20, signifies the â€skyâ€, and is the same word as Camulus, the
Gaulish heavengod identified by the omans with Mars. is followers are
as doubtfully human as himself. ne may compare them with Cuchulainn and
the rest of the heroes of main Macha. heir deeds are not less
marvellous. ike the ltonian warriors, they move, too, on eual terms
with the gods. â€he ianna of rinâ€, says a tract called â€he ialogue
of the ldersâ€,2 contained in thirteenth and fourteenth century

r, Manannn, and odb the ed, with their countless sons and

as do the enians themselves. hey fight for them, or against them they
marry them, and are given to them in marriage.

A luminous suggestion of rofessor hys also hints that the enians
inherited the conduct of that ancient war formerly waged between the
uatha  anann and the omors. he most common antagonists of inn and
his heroes are tribes of invaders from oversea, called in the stories
the ochlannach. hese â€Men of ochlannâ€ are usually identified, by
those who look for history in the stories of the enian cycle, with the
invading bands of orsemen who harried the Irish coasts in the ninth

candinavian raids, and mortal foes have probably merely stepped into

country, not over the seaâ€”but under it.2

he earlier historians of Ireland were as ready with their dates and
facts regarding the enian band as an institution as with the
personality of inn. It was said to have been first organized by a king
called iachadh, in 00 .C., and abolished, or rather, exterminated, by
Cairbr, the son of Cormac mac Art, in 2 A.. We are told that it
consisted of three regiments modelled on the oman legion each of these
bodies contained, on a peace footing, three thousand men, but in time of
war could be indefinitely strengthened. Its obect was to defend the
coasts of Ireland and the country generally, throwing its weight upon
the side of any prince who happened to be assailed by foreign foes.
uring the six months of winter, its members were uartered upon the
population, but during the summer they had to forage for themselves,
which they did by hunting and fishing. hus they lived in the woods and
on the open moors, hardening themselves for battle by their adventurous
life. he sites of their enormous campfires were long pointed out under
the name of the â€eniansâ€ cookingplacesâ€.

It was not easy to become a member of this famous band. A candidate had
to be not only an expert warrior, but a poet and a man of culture as
well. e had practically to renounce his tribe at any rate he made oath
that he would neither avenge any of his relatives nor be avenged by
them. e put himself under bonds never to refuse hospitality to anyone
who asked, never to turn his back in battle, never to insult any woman,
and not to accept a dowry with his wife. In addition to all this, he had
to pass successfully through the most stringent physical tests. Indeed,
as these have come down to us, magnified by the perfervid Celtic
imagination, they are of an altogether marvellous and impossible
character. An aspirant to the ianna irinn, we are told, had first to
stand up to his knees in a pit dug for him, his only arms being his
shield and a hazel wand, while nine warriors, each with a spear,
standing within the distance of nine ridges of land, all hurled their
weapons at him at once if he failed to ward them all off, he was
reected. hould he succeed in this first test, he was given the
distance of one treelengthâ€s start, and chased through a forest by
armed men if any of them came up to him and wounded him, he could not
belong to the enians. If he escaped unhurt, but had unloosed a single
lock of his braided hair, or had broken a single branch in his flight,
or if, at the end of the run, his weapons trembled in his hands, he was
refused. As, besides these tests, he was obliged to ump over a branch
as high as his forehead, and stoop under one as low as his knee, while
running at full speed, and to pluck a thorn out of his heel without
hindrance to his flight, it is clear that even the rank and file of the
enians must have been uite exceptional athletes.2

ut it is time to pass on to a more detailed description of these
champions.2 hey are a goodly company, not less heroic than the
mighty men of lster. irst comes inn himself, not the strongest in
body of the enians, but the truest, wisest, and kindest, gentle to
women, generous to men, and trusted by all. If he could help it, he

A number of spirited ballads2 tell how ssian, stranded in his old

In spite, however, of the widespread popularity of the ballads that
took the form of dialogues between ssian and atrick, certain
traditions say that the saint succeeded in converting the hero. Caoilt,
the other great surviving enian, was also represented as having gladly
exchanged his pagan lore for the faith and salvation offered him. We may
see the same influence on foot in the later legends concerning the ed
ranch Champions. It was the policy of the first Christianizers of
Ireland to describe the loved heroes of their still halfheathen flocks
as having handed in their submission to the new creed. he tales about
Conchobar and Cuchulainn were amended, to prove that those very pagan
personages had been miraculously brought to accept the gospel at the
last. An entirely new story told how the latter hero was raised from the
dead by aint atrick that he might bear witness of the truth of
Christianity to aogaire the econd, ing of Ireland, which he did with
such fervour and elouence that the sceptical monarch was
convinced.20

aring attempts were also made to change the uatha  anann from pagan
gods into Christian saints, but these were by no means so profitable as
the policy pursued towards the more humanseeming heroes. With one of
them alone, was success immediate and brilliant. rigit, the goddess of
fire, poetry, and the hearth, is famous today as aint ridget, or
ride. Most popular of all the Irish saints, she can still be easily
recognized as the daughter of the agda. er Christian attributes,
almost all connected with fire, attest her pagan origin.2 he was
born at sunrise a house in which she dwelt blazed into a flame which
reached to heaven a pillar of fire rose from her head when she took the
veil and her breath gave new life to the dead. As with the ritish
goddess ul, worshipped at ath, whoâ€”the first century atin writer
olinus22 tells usâ€”â€ruled over the boiling springs, and at her altar
there flamed a perpetual fire which never whitened into ashes, but
hardened into a stony massâ€, the sacred flame on her shrine at ildare
was never allowed to go out. It was extinguished once, in the thirteenth
century, but was relighted, and burnt with undying glow until the
suppression of the monasteries by enry the ighth. his sacred fire
might not be breathed on by the impure human breath. or nineteen nights
it was tended by her nuns, but on the twentieth night it was left
untouched, and kept itself alight miraculously. With so little of her
essential character and ritual changed, it is small wonder that the
halfpagan, halfChristian Irish gladly accepted the new saint in the
stead of the old goddess.

oubtless a careful examination of Irish hagiology would result in the
discovery of many other saints whose names and attributes might render
them suspect of previous careers as pagan gods. ut their acceptation
was not sufficiently general to do away with the need of other means of
counteracting the still living influence of the Gaelic antheon.
herefore a fresh school of euhemerists arose to prove that the gods
were never even saints, but merely worldly men who had once lived and
ruled in rin. earned monks worked hard to construct a history of
Ireland from the lood downwards. Mr. ugene â€Curry has compiled from
the various pedigrees they elaborated, and inserted into the books of

merely the uatha  anann, but also the ir olgs, the omors, the
Milesians, and the races of artholon and emed were descended from
oah. aphet, the patriarchâ€s son, was the father of Magog, from whom
came two lines, the first being the Milesians, while the second branched
out into all the other races.2

aving once worked the gods, first into universal history, and then into
the history of Ireland, it was an easy matter to supply them with dates
of birth and death, local habitations, and places of burial. We are told
with precision exactly how long uada, the agda, ugh, and the others
reigned at ara. he barrows by the oyne provided them with comfortable
tombs. heir enemies, the omors, became real invaders who were beaten
in real battles. hus it was thought to make plain prose of their
divinities.

It is only fair, however, to these early euhemerists to say that they
have their modern disciples. here are many writers, of recognized
authority upon their subects, who, in dealing with the history of

under the suspicion of having been Iberians, Moors, omans, inns,
Goths, or eutons. As for the people of artholon and emed, they have
even been explained as men of the alolithic Age. his chaos of opinion
was fortunately avoided by the native annalists, who had no particular
views upon the uestion of race, except that everybody came from
â€painâ€.

Gobhan the Architect and his son, young Gobhan, runs the tale, were sent

the top of the hill, and thence dispersed among the fields, waving these

friendly, and, indeed, more than friendly, to men. Whether or not she
were the mother of the gods, she is claimed as first ancestress by half
a dozen famous Irish families.

Among her children was the famous arl Gerald, offspring of her alliance
with the fourth arl of esmond, known as â€he Magicianâ€. As in the
wellknown story of the wanmaidens, the magicianearl is said to have
stolen Ainâ€s cloak while she was bathing, and refused to return it

she had lost her husband, she was not separated from her son, who was
received into the fairy world after his death, and now lives under the
surface of ough Gur, in County imerick, waiting, like the ritish
Arthur, for the hour to strike in which he shall lead forth his warriors

ot only the tribe of anu, but heroes of the other mythical cycles

ut besides these figures, which can be traced in mythology or history,
and others who, though all written record of them has perished, are
obviously of the same character, there are numerous beings who suggest a
different origin from that of the Aryanseeming fairies. hey correspond
to the elves and trolls of candinavian, or the silenoi and satyrs of

knows where hidden treasures are the Gan Ceanach, or â€lovetalkerâ€, who

travellers astray, or, taking the shape of an ass or mule, beguiles them
to mount upon his back to their discomfiture the ulachan, who rides
without a head and other friendly or malicious sprites. Whence come
they A possible answer suggests itself. receding the Aryans, and
surviving the Aryan conuest all over urope, was a large nonAryan
population, which must have had its own gods, who would retain their
worship, be revered by successive generations, and remain rooted to the
soil. May not these uncouth and halfdeveloped Irish eprechauns,

figures of those darker gods of â€the dark Iberiansâ€

he descriptions and the stories of the ritish gods have hardly come

It is with the family of wyll, deities connected with the southwest
corner of Wales, called by the omans emetia, and by the ritons yfed,
and, roughly speaking, identical with the modern county of
embrokeshire, that the earliest consecutive accounts of the ritish
gods begin. he first of the our ranches of the Mabinogi tell us how
â€wyll, rince of yfedâ€, gained the right to be called en Annwn, the
â€ead of adesâ€. Indeed, it almost seems as if it had been deliberately
written to explain how the same person could be at once a mere mortal
prince, however legendary, and a ruler in the mystic ther World, and so
to reconcile two conflicting traditions.0 ut to an earlier age than
that in which the legend was put into a literary shape, such forced
reconciliation would not have been needed for the two legends would not
have been considered to conflict. When wyll, head of Annwn, was a
mythic person whose tradition was still alive, the unexplored, rugged,
and savage country of yfed, populated by the aboriginal Iberians whom
the Celt had driven into such remote districts, appeared to those who
dwelt upon the eastern side of its dividing river, the aw, at least a
dependency of Annwn, if not that weird realm itself. ut, as men grew
bolder, the frontier was crossed, and yfed entered and traversed, and
found to be not so unlike other countries. Its inhabitants, if not of
Celtic race, were yet of flesh and blood. o that, though the province
still continued to bear to a late date the names of the â€and of
Illusionâ€ and the â€ealm of Glamourâ€, it was no longer deemed to be
ades itself. hat fitful and shadowy country had folded its tents, and
departed over or under seas.

he story of â€wyll, rince of yfedâ€,2 tells us how there was war
in Annwn between its two kingsâ€”or between two, perhaps, of its many
chieftains. Arawn â€ilverongueâ€ and avgan â€ummerWhiteâ€ each
coveted the dominions of the other. In the continual contests between
them, Arawn was worsted, and in despair he visited the upper earth to
seek for a mortal ally.

At this time wyll, rince of yfed, held his court at arberth. e had,
however, left his capital upon a hunting expedition to Glyn Cch, known
today as a valley upon the borders of the two counties of embroke and
Carmarthen. ike so many kings of uropean and riental romance, when an
adventure is at hand, he became separated from his party, and was, in
modern parlance, â€thrown outâ€. e could, however, still hear the music
of his hounds, and was listening to them, when he also distinguished the
cry of another pack coming towards him. As he watched and listened, a
stag came into view and the strange hounds pulled it down almost at his
feet. At first wyll hardly looked at the stag, he was so taken up with
gazing at the hounds, for â€of all the hounds that he had seen in the
world, he had never seen any that were like unto these. or their hair
was of a brilliant shining white, and their ears were red and as the
whiteness of their bodies shone, so did the redness of their ears

While he was doing this, a man â€upon a large, lightgray steed, with a
huntinghorn round his neck, and clad in garments of gray woollen in the

wyll expressed his contrition, and, asking the newcomerâ€s name and

arberth.

o one in Annwn suspected wyll of being anyone else than their king. e

he second mythological incident in the Mabinogi of wyll, rince of

Manawyddan was now the sole survivor of the family of lyr. e was

Manawyddan could make saddles, and he made them so well that soon no one

he â€Coming of Arthurâ€, his sudden rise into prominence, is one of the

he most probable, and only adeuate explanation, is given by rofessor

accidentally confused, to the exceeding renown of a composite,

It is in keeping with the mythological character of Arthur that the
early Welsh tales recorded of him are of a different nature from those
which swell the pseudohistories of ennius and of Geoffrey of
Monmouth. We hear nothing of that subugation of the countries of
Western urope which fills so large a part in the two books of the
istoria ritonum which Geoffrey has devoted to him. Conueror he
is, but his conuests are not in any land known to geographers. It is
against ades, and not against ome, that he achieves his highest
triumphs. his is the true history of ing Arthur, and we may read more
fragments and snatches of it in two prosetales preserved in the ed
ook of ergest. oth these tales date, in the actual form in which they
have come down to us, from the twelfth century. ut, in each of them,
the writer seems to be stretching out his hands to gather in the dying
traditions of a very remote past.

When a Welsh manatarms named honabwy lay down, one night, to sleep
upon a yellow calfskin, the only furniture in a noisome hut, in which
he had taken shelter, that was comparatively free from vermin, he had
the vision which is related in the tale called â€he ream of
honabwyâ€. e thought that he was travelling with his companions
towards the evern, when they heard a rushing noise behind them, and,
looking back, saw a gigantic rider upon a monstrous horse. o terrible
was the horsemanâ€s appearance that they all started to run from him. ut
their running was of no avail, for every time the horse drew in its
breath, it sucked them back to its very chest, only, however, to fling
them forward as it breathed out again. In despair they fell down and
besought their pursuerâ€s mercy. e granted it, asked their names, and
told them, in return, his own. e was known as Iddawc the Agitator of
