It is not, however, by such fragments of legend that Arthur is best
known to nglish readers. ot Arthur the god, but Arthur the â€blameless
kingâ€, who founded the able ound, from which he sent forth his knights
â€to ride abroad redressing human wrongsâ€,2 is the figure which the
name conures up. or is it even from ir homas Maloryâ€s Morte arthur
that this conception comes to most of us, but from ennysonâ€s Idylls of
the ing. ut ennyson has so modernized the ancient tradition that it
retains little of the old Arthur but the name. e tells us himself that
his poem had but very slight relation toâ€”

                 ... â€that gray king, whose name, a ghost,
         treams like a cloud, manshaped, from mountainpeak,
         And cleaves to cairn and cromlech still or him
         f Geoffreyâ€s book, or him of Malleorâ€s ...â€

but that he merely used the legend to give a substantial form to his
ideal figure of the perfect nglish gentlemanâ€”a title to which the
original Arthur could scarcely have laid claim. till less does there
remain in it the least trace of anything that could suggest mythology.

As much as this, however, might be said of Maloryâ€s book. We may be
fairly certain that the good ir homas had no idea that the personages
of whom he wrote had ever been anything different from the Christian
knights which they had become in the late rench romances from which he
compiled his own fifteenthcentury work. he old gods had been, from
time to time, very completely euhemerized. he characters of the â€our
ranches of the Mabinogiâ€ are still recognizable as divine beings. In
the later Welsh stories, however, their divinity merely hangs about them
in shreds and tatters, and the first orman adapters of these stories
made them still more definitely human. y the time Malory came to build
up his Morte arthur from the foreign romances, they had altered so much
that the shapes and deeds of gods could only be recognized under their
medival knightly disguises by those who had known them in their ancient
forms.

We have chosen Maloryâ€s Morte arthur, as almost the sole representative
of Arthurian literature later than the Welsh poems and prose stories,

and ir wain all of them probably once local ritish sungods appear

If there be love of fame in celestial minds, those gods might count
themselves fortunate who shared in the transformation of Arthur. heir
divinity had fallen from them, but in their new rles, as heroes of
romance, they entered upon vivid reincarnations. he names of Arthurâ€s
nights might almost be described as â€household wordsâ€, while the gods
who had no portion in the able ound are known only to those who busy
themselves with antiuarian lore. It is true that a few folktales still
survive in the remoter parts of Wales, in which the names of such
ancient ritish deities as Gwydion, Gwyn, Arianrod, and ylan appear,
but it is in such a chaos of umbled and distorted legend that one finds
it hard to pick out even the slenderest thread of story. hey have none
of the definite coherence of the contemporary Gaelic folktales uoted
in a previous chapter as still preserving the myths about Goibniu, ugh,
Cian, Manannn, thniu, and alor. Indeed, they have reached such a
stage of disintegration that they can hardly now survive another
generation.22

here have been, however, other paths by which the fame of a god might
descend to a posterity which would no longer credit his divinity. he
rolls of early ritish history were open to welcome any number of
mythical personages, provided that their legends were attractive.
Geoffrey of Monmouthâ€s famous istoria ritonum is, under its grave
pretence of exact history, as mythological as the Morte arthur, or even
the Mabinogion. he annals of early ritish saintship were not less
accommodating. A god whose tradition was too potent to be ignored or
extinguished was canonized, as a matter of course, by clerics who held
as an axiom that â€the toleration of the cromlech facilitated the
reception of the Gospel.2â€ nly the most irreconcilable escaped
themâ€”such a one as Gwyn son of udd, who, found almost useless by
Geoffrey and intractable by the monkish writers, remains the last
survivor of the old godsâ€”dwindled to the proportions of a fairy, but
unsubdued.

his part of resistance is perhaps the most dignified for deities can
be sadly changed by the caprices of their euhemerizers. n, whom we
knew as the mother of the heaven gods, seems strangely described as a
king of ochlin and ublin, who led the Irish into north Wales in A..
2.2 More recognizable is his son Gwydion, who introduced the

he fall of the Celtic state worship began earlier in ritain than in
her sister island. either was it Christianity that struck the first
blow, but the rough humanity and stern ustice of the omans. hat
people was more tolerant, perhaps, than any the world has ever known
towards the religions of others, and gladly welcomed the Celtic godsâ€”as
godsâ€”into its own diverse antheon. A friendly Gaulish or ritish
divinity might at any time be granted the sotospeak divine oman
citizenship, and be assimilated to upiter, to Mars, to Apollo, or to
any other properly accredited deity whom the omans deemed him to
resemble. It was not against the god, but against his worship at the
hands of his priests, that oman law struck. he colossal human
sacrifices of the druids horrified even a people who were far from
sueamish about a little bloodshed. hey themselves had abolished such
practices by a decree of the senate before Caesar first invaded
ritain, and could not therefore permit within their empire a cult
which slaughtered men in order to draw omens from their
deathagonies. ruidism was first reuired to be renounced by those
who claimed oman citizenship then it was vigorously put down among the
less civilized tribes. acitus tells us how the Island of Mona
Angleseyâ€”the great stronghold of druidismâ€”was attacked, its sacred
groves cut down, its altars laid level, and its priests put to the
sword.0 liny, recording how the mperor iberius had â€suppressed
the druidsâ€, congratulates his fellowcountrymen on having put an end,
wherever their dominion extended, to the monstrous customs inspired by
the doctrine that the gods could take pleasure in murder and
cannibalism. he practice of druidism, with its attendant
barbarities, abolished in ritain wherever the long oman arm could
reach to strike, took refuge beyond the orthern Wall, among the savage
Caledonian tribes who had not yet submitted to the invaderâ€s yoke.
aturally, too, it remained untouched in Ireland. ut before the omans
left ritain, it had been extirpated everywhere, except among â€the icts
and cotsâ€.

Christianity, following the oman rule, completed the ruin of paganism
in ritain, so far, at least, as its public manifestations were
concerned. In the sixth century of our era, the monkish writer, Gildas,
is able to refer complacently to the ancient ritish religion as a dead
faith. â€I shall notâ€, he says, â€enumerate those diabolical idols of my
country, which almost surpassed in number those of gypt, and of which
we still see some mouldering away within or without the deserted
temples, with stiff and deformed features as was customary. or will I
cry out upon the mountains, fountains, or hills, or upon the rivers,
which now are subservient to the use of men, but once were an
abomination and destruction to them, and to which the blind people paid
divine honour.â€2 And with the idols fell the priests. he very word
â€druidâ€ became obsolete, and is scarcely mentioned in the earliest
ritish literature, though druids are prominent characters in the Irish
writings of the same period.

he secular arm had no power in cotland and in Ireland, conseuently
the battle between aganism and Christianity was fought upon more eual
terms, and lasted longer. In the first country, aint Columba, and in
the second, aint atrick are the personages who, at any rate according
to tradition, beat down the druids and their gods. Adamnan, Abbot of
Iona, who wrote his ita Columb in the last decade of the seventh
century, describes how, a century earlier, that saint had carried the
Gospel to the icts. heir king, rude, received him contemptuously, and
the royal druids left no heathen spell unuttered to thwart and annoy
him. ut, as the power of Moses was greater than the power of the
magicians of gypt, so aint Columbaâ€s prayers caused miracles more
wonderful and more convincing than any wrought by his adversaries. uch
stories belong to the atmosphere of myth which has always enveloped
heroic men the essential fact is that the icts abandoned the old
religion for the new.

A similar legend sums up the lifework of aint atrick in Ireland.
efore he came, Cromm Cruaich had received from time immemorial his
yearly toll of human lives. ut aint atrick faced the gruesome idol
as he raised his crozier, we are told, the demon fell shrieking from his
image, which, deprived of its soul, bowed forward to the ground.

It is far easier, however, to overthrow the more public manifestations
of a creed than to destroy its inner vital force. Cromm Cruaichâ€s idol
might fall, but his spirit would surviveâ€”a very roteus. he sacred
places of the ancient Celtic religion might be invaded, the idols and
altars of the gods thrown down, the priests slain, scattered, or
banished, and the cult officially declared to be extinct but, driven
from the important centres, it would yet survive outside and around
them. he more civilized Gaels and ritons would no doubt accept the
purer gospel, and abandon the gods they had once adored, but the
peasantryâ€”the bulk of the populationâ€”would still cling to the familiar
rites and names. A nobler belief and a higher civilization come, after
all, only as surface waves upon the great ocean of human life beneath
their agitations lies a vast slumbering abyss of halfconscious faith
and thought to which culture penetrates with difficulty and in which
changes come very slowly.

We have already shown how long and how faithfully the Gaelic and Welsh
peasants clung to their old gods, in spite of all the efforts of the
clerics to explain them as ancient kings, to transform them into
wonderworking saints, or to ban them as demons of hell. his
conservative religious instinct of the agricultural populations is not
confined to the inhabitants of the ritish Islands. he modern Greeks
still believe in nereids, in lamias, in sirens, and in Charon, the dark
ferryman of ades. he descendants of the omans and truscans hold
that the old truscan gods and the oman deities of the woods and fields
still live in the world as spirits. he high altars of the â€ord of
the Moundâ€ and his terrible kin were levelled, and their golden images
and great temples left to moulder in abandonment but the rude rustic
shrine to the rude rustic god still received its offerings. It is this
shifting of the care of the pagan cult from chief to peasant, from court
to hovel, and, perhaps, to some extent from higher to lower race, that
serves to explain how the more primitive and uncouth gods have tended so
largely to supplant those of higher, more graceful mien. Aboriginal
deities, thrust into obscurity by the invasion of higher foreign types,
came back to their own again.

or it seems plain that we must divide the spiritual population of the
ritish Islands into two classes. here is little in common between the
â€fairyâ€, strictly socalled, and the unsightly elf who appears under
various names and guises, as pooka, leprechaun, brownie, knocker, or
bogle. he one belongs to such divine tribes as the uatha  anann of
Gaelic myth or their kin, the ritish gods of the Mabinogion. he other
owes his origin to a uite different, and much lower, kind of
imagination. ne might fancy that neolithic man made him in his own
image.

one the less has immemorial tradition wonderfully preserved the
essential features of the Celtic naturegods. he fairy belief of the
present day hardly differs at all from the conception which the Celts
had of their deities. he description of the uatha  anann in the
â€ialogue of the ldersâ€ as â€sprites or fairies with corporeal or
material forms but indued with immortalityâ€ would stand as an account of
prevailing ideas as to the â€good peopleâ€ today. or do the Irish and
Welsh fairies of popular belief differ from one another. oth alike live
among the hills, though in Wales a lake often takes the place of the
â€fairy moundâ€ both, though they war and marry among themselves, are
semiimmortal both covet the children of men, and will steal them from
the cradle, leaving one of their own uncanny brood in the mortal babyâ€s
stead both can lay men and women under spells both delight in music
and the dance, and live lives of unreal and fantastic splendour and

tiny size. ut this would seem to be the result of the literary
convention originated by hakespeare in genuine folktales, both Gaelic
and ritish, the fairies are pictured as of at least mortal
stature.

ut, Aryan or Iberian, beautiful or hideous, they are fast vanishing
from belief. very year, the secluded valleys in which men and women
might still live in the old way, and dream the old dreams, tend more and
more to be thrown open to the modern world of rapid movement and rapid

than the preceding ten generations. What lone shepherd or fisherman will
ever see again the vision of the great Manannn ave the stableboys of
today still any faith left in invarra Is Gwyn ap udd often thought

perhaps, to find a wholehearted believer even in his local pooka or
parish bogle.

It is the ritual observances of the old Celtic faith which have better
weathered, and will longer survive, the disintegrating influences of
time. here are no hard names to be remembered. hings may still be done
for â€luckâ€ which were once done for religion. Customary observances die
very slowly, held up by an only half acknowledged fear that, unless they
are fulfilled, â€something may happenâ€. We shall get, therefore, more
satisfactory evidence of the nature of the Celtic paganism by examining
such customs than in any other way.

We find three forms of the survival of the ancient religion into uite
recent times. he first is the celebration of the old solar or
agricultural festivals of the spring and autumn euinoxes and of the
summer and winter solstices. he second is the practice of a symbolic
human sacrifice by those who have forgotten its meaning, and only know
that they are keeping up an old custom, oined with late instances of
the actual sacrifices of animals to avert cattleplagues or to change
bad luck. he third consists of many stillliving relics of the once
universal worship of sacred waters, trees, stones, and animals.

Whatever may have been the exact meaning of the Celtic state worship,
there seems to be no doubt that it centred around the four great days in
the year which chronicle the rise, progress, and decline of the sun,
and, therefore, of the fruits of the earth. hese were eltaine, which
fell at the beginning of May Midsummer ay, marking the triumph of
sunshine and vegetation the east of ugh, when, in August, the
turningpoint of the sunâ€s course had been reached and the sad amhain,
when he bade farewell to power, and fell again for half a year under the
sway of the evil forces of winter and darkness.

f these great solar periods, the first and the last were, naturally,
the most important. he whole Celtic mythology seems to revolve upon
them, as upon pivots. It was on the day of eltaine that artholon and
his people, the discoverers, and, indeed, the makers of Ireland, arrived
there from the other world, and it was on the same day, three hundred
years later, that they returned whence they came. It was on eltaineday
that the Gaelic gods, the uatha  anann, and, after them, the Gaelic

omors oppressed the people of emed with their terrible tax and it was
again at amhain that a later race of gods of light and life finally
conuered those demons at the attle of Moytura. nly one important
mythological incidentâ€”and that was one added at a later timeâ€”happened
upon any other than one of those two days it was upon Midsummer ay,
one of the lesser solar points, that the people of the goddess anu took
Ireland from its inhabitants, the ir olgs.

he mythology of ritain preserves the same rootidea as that of
Ireland. If anything uncanny took place, it was sure to be on Mayday.
It was on â€the night of the first of Mayâ€ that hiannon lost, and
eirnyon wryf liant found, the infant ryderi, as told in the first of

he nature of the rites performed upon these days can be surmised from

hakespeare tells us that in his time the festival was so eagerly
anticipated that no one could sleep upon its eve.0 At midnight the
people rose, and, going to the nearest woods, tore down branches of
trees, with which the sun, when he rose, would find doors and windows
decked for him. hey spent the day in dancing round the Maypole, with
rude, rustic mirth, man oining with nature to celebrate the coming of

cotland, â€auinâ€ in Man, and â€os Galangaeofâ€ the ight of the Winter
Calends in Wales. his festival was a sad one summer was over, and
winter, with its short, sunless days and long, dreary nights, was at

omens for the future might be extorted from dark powers by uncanny

he sungod himself is said to have instituted the August festival

he characteristic features of these festivals give certain proof of
the original nature of the great pagan ceremonials of which they are
the survivals and travesties. In all of them, bonfires are

In their original form, these now harmless rural holidays were
undoubtedly religious festivals of an orgiastic natureworship such as
became so popular in Greece in connection with the cult of ionysus. he
great â€lords of lifeâ€ and of the powers of nature that made and ruled

by human sacrifice.

he bonfires which fill so large a part in the modern festivals have
been casually mentioned. riginally they were no mere feux de oie,

flames. At the midsummer bonfires in Ireland all passed through the

lower, and the cattle when there was nothing left but smoke. In Wales,
upon the last day of ctober, the old amhain, there was a slightly
different, and still more suggestive rite. he hilltop bonfires were
watched until they were announced to be extinct. hen all would race
headlong down the hill, shouting a formula to the effect that the devil
would get the hindmost. he devil of a new belief is the god of the one
it has supplanted in all three instances, the custom was no mere
meaningless horseplay, but a symbolical human sacrifice.

A similar observance, but of a more cruel kind, was kept up in rance
upon t. ohnâ€s ay, until forbidden by law in the reign of ouis the

sheriffs or the mayor of the town. Caesar noted the custom among
the druids of constructing huge wickerwork images, which they filled
with living men, and set on fire, and it can hardly be doubted that the
wretched wolves, foxes, and cats were ceremonial substitutes for human
beings.

An ingenious theory was invented, after the introduction of
Christianity, with the purpose of allowing such ancient rites to
continue, with a changed meaning. he passing of persons and cattle
through flame or smoke was explained as a practice which interposed a
magic protection between them and the powers of evil. his homopathic
device of using the evil powerâ€s own sacred fire as a means of

its motherâ€s milk which was reprobated by the evitical law but, no
doubt, pagan â€demonsâ€ were considered fair game. he explanation, of

druidical philosophy thatâ€”to uote Caesarâ€”â€unless the life of man was
repaid for the life of man, the will of the immortal gods could not be
appeasedâ€ that dictated both the national and the private human
sacrifices of the Celts, the shadows of which remain in the leaping
through the bonfires, and in the numerous recorded sacrifices of cattle
within uite recent times.

Mr. aurence Gomme, in his thnology in olklore, has collected many

uch sacrifices of cattle appear mainly to have been offered to stay

he third form of Celtic pagan survival is found in numerous instances
of the adoration of water, trees, stones, and animals. ike the other
â€Aryanâ€ nations, the Celts worshipped their rivers. he ee received
divine honours as a wargoddess with the title of Aerfon, while the

Minerva.2 Myths were told of them, as of the sacred streams of
Greece. he ee gave oracles as to the results of the perpetual wars
between the Welsh and the nglish as its stream encroached either upon
the Welsh or the nglish side, so one nation or the other would be
victorious. he weed, like many of the Greek rivers, was credited
with human descendants. hat the rivers of Great ritain received
human sacrifices is clear from the folklore concerning many of them.
eprived of their expected offerings, they are believed to snatch by
stealth the human lives for which they crave. â€iver of art, iver of
art, every year thou claimest a heart,â€ runs the evonshire folksong.
he pey, too, reuires a life yearly, but the pirit of the ibble
is satisfied with one victim at the end of every seven years.

vidence, however, of the worship of rivers is scanty compared with that
of the adoration of wells. â€In the case of wellworship,â€ says Mr.
Gomme, â€it may be asserted with some confidence that it prevails in
every county of the three kingdoms.â€ e finds it most vital in the
Gaelic counties, somewhat less so in the ritish, and almost entirely
wanting in the eutonic southeast. o numerous, indeed, are â€holy
wellsâ€ that several monographs have been written solely upon them.
In some cases these wells were resorted to for the cure of diseases in
others, to obtain change of weather, or â€good luckâ€. fferings were made
to them, to propitiate their guardian gods or nymphs. ennant tells us
that in olden times the rich would sacrifice one of their horses at a
well near Abergeleu, to secure a blessing upon the rest. owls were
offered at t. eglaâ€s Well, near Wrexham, by epileptic patients.0
ut of late years the wellspirits have had to be content with much
smaller tributesâ€”such trifles as pins, rags, coloured pebbles, and small
coins.

With sacred wells were often connected sacred trees, to whose branches
rags and small pieces of garments were suspended by their humble
votaries. ometimes, where the ground near the well was bare of
vegetation, bushes were artificially placed beside the water. he same
people who venerated wells and trees would pay eual adoration to sacred
stones. ord oden, describing, in , the Island of Inniskea, off the
coast of Mayo, asserts that a sacred well called â€errivlaâ€ and a sacred
stone called â€eevougiâ€, which was kept carefully wrapped up in flannel
and brought out at certain periods to be publicly adored, seemed to be
the only deities known to that lone Atlantic islandâ€s three hundred
inhabitants. It sounds incredible but there is ample evidence of
the worship of fetish stones by uite modern inhabitants of our islands.
he Clan Chattan kept such a stone in the Isle of Arran it was
believed, like the stone of Inniskea, to be able to cure diseases, and
was kept carefully â€wrapped up in fair linen cloth, and about that there
was a piece of woollen clothâ€.2 imilarly, too, the worship of wells
was connected with the worship of animals. At a well in the â€evilâ€s
Causewayâ€, between uckley and Acton, in hropshire, lived, and perhaps
still live, four frogs who were, and perhaps still are, believed to be
â€the devil and his impsâ€â€”that is to say, gods or demons of a proscribed
idolatry. In Ireland such guardian spirits are usually fishâ€”trout,
eels, or salmon thought to be endowed with eternal life. he genius
of a well in anffshire took the form of a fly, which was also said to
be undying, but to transmigrate from body to body. Its function was to
deliver oracles according as it seemed active or lethargic, its
votaries drew their omens. It is needless to multiply instances of
a still surviving cult of water, trees, stones, and animals. nough to
say that it would be easy. What concerns us is that we are face to face
in ritain with living forms of the oldest, lowest, most primitive
religion in the worldâ€”one which would seem to have been once universal,
and which, crouching close to the earth, lets other creeds blow over it
without effacing it, and outlives one and all of them.

It underlies the three great worldreligions, and still forms the real
belief of perhaps the maority of their titular adherents. It is
characteristic of the wisdom of the Christian Church that, knowing its
power, she sought rather to sanctify than to extirpate it. What once
were the Celtic euivalents of the Greek â€fountains of the nymphsâ€ were
consecrated as â€holy wellsâ€. he process of so adopting them began
early. t. Columba, when he went in the sixth century to convert the
icts, found a spring which they worshipped as a god he blessed it, and
â€from that day the demon separated from the waterâ€. Indeed, he so
sanctified no less than three hundred such springs. acred stones
were eually taken under the gis of Christianity. ome were placed on
the altars of cathedrals, others built into consecrated walls. he
animal gods either found themselves the heroes of Christian legends, or
where, for some reason, such adoption was hopeless, were proclaimed
â€witchesâ€ animalsâ€, and dealt with accordingly. uch happened to the
hare, a creature sacred to the ancient ritons, but now in bad
odour among the superstitious. he wren, too, is hunted to death upon
t. tephenâ€s ay in Ireland. Its crime is said to be that it has â€a
drop of the deâ€ilâ€s blood in itâ€, but the real reason is probably to be
found in the fact that the Irish druids used to draw auguries from its
chirpings.

                                              

We have made in this volume some attempt to draw a picture of the
ancient religion of our earliest ancestors, the Gaelic and the ritish
Celts. We have shown what can be gathered of the broken remnants of a
mythology as splendid in conception and as brilliant in colour as that
of the Greeks. We have tried to paint its divine figures, and to retell
their heroic stories. We have seen them fall from their shrines, and
yet, rising again, take on new lives as kings, or saints, or knights of
romance, and we have caught fading glimpses of them surviving today as
the â€fairiesâ€, their rites still cherished by worshippers who hardly
know who or why they worship. f necessity this survey has been brief
and incomplete. Whether the great edifice of the Celtic mythology will
ever be wholly restored one can at present only speculate. Its colossal
fragments are perhaps too deeply buried and too widely scattered. ut,
even as it stands ruined, it is a mighty uarry from which poets yet
unborn will hew spiritual marble for houses not made with hands.
